⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنفرُ الذي ولي الخلافة العظمى الخلفاء الأربعة، فتولاها أبو بكر رضي الله عنه سنتين وثلاثة أشهر وعشرة أيام، وتولاها عمر رضي الله عنه عشر سنين وستة أشهر وثمانية أيام، وتولاها عثمان رضي الله عنه إحدى عشرة سنة وأحد عشر شهراً وتسعة أيام، وتولاها علي رضي الله عنه وكرَّم الله وجهه أربع سنين وتسعة أشهر وسبعة أيام، فالمجموع تسعة وعشرون سنة وستة أشهر وأربعة أيام فلم تَكْمُل المدَّة التي قدَّرها النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلا بأيام الحسن بن علي رضي الله عنهما كذا حرَّره السيوطي، لذا قال معاوية: (أنا أول الملوك، وإلى هذا التفصيل ذهب الجمهور، خلافاً لما نقله طائفة المازني عن من عدم المفاضلة بين الصحابة . قوله: (وَأَمْرُهُم في الفَضْل (كالخِلافَة :أي: وشأن الخلفاء الأربعة في ترتيبهم في الفضل بمعنى كثرة الثواب على حسب ترتيبهم في الخلافة عند أهل السنة، فأفضلهم أبو بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ثم عليّ رضي الله عنهم، ويدلُّ لذلك حديث ابن عمر: كنا نقول ورسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يسمعُ : خيرُ هذه الأمة بعد نبيها : أبو بكر، ثم عمر، عثمان، ثم علي رضي الله عنهم، فلم يَنْهَنَا، وقد قال السعد على هذا وجدنا السلف والخلف والظاهر أنه لو لم يكن لهم دليل على ذلك لما حكموا به، وفي ذلك رد على الخطابية؛ وهم فرقة تنسب لابن الخطاب الأسدي، تقول بتقديم عمر، وفيه رد على الراوندية، وكانوا في الأصل يُقال لهم: العباسية، يقولون بتقديم العباس ابن عبد المطلب، وإنما غير اسمهم؛ لئلا يتوهم أنهم أولاد العباس. وفيه ردُّ أيضاً على الشّيعة - بفتح الياء - وهم فرقة تتغالي في حب سيدنا علي رضي الله عنه، فتقدمُهُ على سائر الصحابة، وأما أهل الكوفة وبعض أهل السنة وجمهور المعتزلة وسيدنا مالك في قوله الأول .. فيقدمون عليا على عثمان فقط، ففرق بين قول الشيعة وقول هؤلاء، وإن أوهم كلام الشارح خلاف ذلك.
And the group that assumed the supreme caliphate are the four caliphs. Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, assumed it for two years, three months, and ten days. Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, assumed it for ten years, six months, and eight days. Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, assumed it for eleven years, eleven months, and nine days. Ali, may Allah be pleased with him and honor his face, assumed it for four years, nine months, and seven days. The total is twenty-nine years, six months, and four days. So the period that the Prophet, peace be upon him, estimated was not completed except by the days of al-Hasan ibn Ali, may Allah be pleased with them both. Thus al-Suyuti recorded it. Hence Mu'awiyah said: 'I am the first of the kings.' The majority held this detailed view, contrary to what a group from al-Mazini reported about those who denied differentiation among the Companions. His words: 'And their matter in virtue is like the caliphate': meaning the affair of the four caliphs in their order in virtue, in the sense of abundance of reward, according to their order in the caliphate according to Ahl al-Sunnah. So the most virtuous among them is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali, may Allah be pleased with them. The hadith of Ibn Umar indicates that: 'We used to say while the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was listening: The best of this nation after its Prophet is Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali, may Allah be pleased with them,' and he did not forbid us. Al-Sa'd said: 'On this we found the predecessors and successors, and it is apparent that if they had no proof for it, they would not have judged by it.' In this is a refutation of al-Khattabiyyah, a sect attributed to Ibn al-Khattab al-Asadi, who say Umar is to be preferred. Also a refutation of al-Rawandiyyah, who were originally called al-Abbasiyyah, and they say al-Abbas ibn Abd al-Muttalib is to be preferred, and they changed their name so that it would not be thought they are the children of al-Abbas. Also a refutation of al-Shi'ah (with a fatha on the ya) who are a sect that exaggerate in love of our master Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, so they prefer him over all the Companions. As for the people of Kufa, some of Ahl al-Sunnah, the majority of the Mu'tazilah, and our master Malik in his first opinion, they prefer Ali only over Uthman. So there is a difference between the statement of the Shi'ah and the statement of these, even if the words of the commentator might give the contrary impression.

The Six Companions Completing the Ten
Lists the six Companions completing the ten and notes no hierarchy among them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the names of the six Companions who complete the ten promised Paradise and understand why no hierarchy is affirmed among them.

Core Idea
The six Companions complete the ten, but their rank is known only from revelation.

The poem of Tuhfat al-Mureed mentions the six Companions who complete the group of ten promised Paradise. This passage from the commentary identifies them by name and clarifies a crucial doctrinal point: we cannot affirm superiority among them without explicit textual evidence. Understanding this teaches us the principle of relying on revelation (tawqif) rather than personal opinion in matters of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the lexical explanations before the list.
2
Identify the six names
The passage lists six Companions. Find each name and make sure you can recall them. They complete the ten promised Paradise.
The first four (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) were mentioned earlier.
3
Understand the doctrinal point
The author states that no text (nass) establishes superiority among these six. Reflect on why this matters: we only affirm what revelation says.
This prevents speculation and innovation in beliefs about the Companions.
4
Apply the principle of tawqif
Consider the broader principle: matters of faith are based on revealed knowledge, not personal reasoning. How does this apply here?
This is a core principle in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَلِيهِمُ قَومٌ كِرامْ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتٌ تَمامُ العَشَرَة قوله : (يَلِيهِمُ بالإشباع ؛ أي: يلي آخرهم وهو عليّ، فالكلام على تقدير مضاف . وقوله : (قوم) أي: رجال . وقوله : (كِرَامٌ) جمع كريم ؛ وهو كريم النفس رفيع النسب. وقوله : (بَرَرَهُ) جمع بَارٌ ؛ وهو المحسن من البر؛ وهو الإحسان. وقوله : ( عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتّ تَمامُ العَشَرة) أي: عددهم سِتَّةٌ تمام العشرة المبشرين بالجنة، فمن جملتهم المشايخ الأربعة السابقون والستة الباقية طلحة بن عبيد الله، والزبير بن : العوام، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص وسعيد بن زيد، وأبو عبيدة عامر بن الجراح ولم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض في الأفضلية، فلا نقول به لعدم التوقيف .
They are followed by a noble, righteous people, their number is six, completing the ten. His words: (They are followed) with the alif of completion: meaning, after the last of them, who is Ali. So the speech involves a suppressed genitive. His words: (a people) meaning men. His words: (noble) plural of generous; he is generous of soul and high in lineage. His words: (righteous) plural of righteous; he is the doer of good from righteousness; it is goodness. His words: (their number is six completing the ten) meaning their number is six, completing the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise. Among them are the four previous masters and the remaining six: Talhah ibn Ubayd Allah, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubaydah Amir ibn al-Jarrah. No text has come regarding the superiority of some over others, so we do not affirm it due to lack of textual evidence.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage explains the line of the poem that mentions the six remaining companions who complete the ten promised Paradise. It begins by parsing the line: 'They are followed' means after the last of the four caliphs, Ali. The author explains each word: 'a people' means men, 'noble' (kiram) means generous in soul and high lineage, 'righteous' (bararah) is plural of barr, meaning the one who does good from righteousness (birr), which is goodness. Then he explains 'their number is six completing the ten,' meaning these six are the remaining ones who complete the list of the ten Companions given glad tidings of Paradise. He lists them: Talhah ibn Ubayd Allah, al-Zubayr ibn al-Awwam, Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu Ubaydah Amir ibn al-Jarrah. The author then clarifies a key doctrinal point: since there is no explicit text (nass) stating that one of these six is superior to another, we do not affirm any hierarchy among them. This is important because it prevents speculation and innovation in matters of faith. The author's final phrase 'li'adam al-tawqif' (due to lack of textual evidence) emphasizes that such judgments must be based on revealed knowledge (tawqif), not personal opinion.

عبد الرحمن بن عوف
سعد بن أبي وقاص
سعيد بن زيد
أبو عبيدة عامر بن الجراح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ten Companions promised Paradise have a known order of superiority, especially among the six additional ones.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for these six, no text establishes superiority, so we do not affirm any order. The only ranking is from revelation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say we do not affirm superiority among the six additional Companions?
A) Because they were all equal in deeds
B) Because there is no textual evidence (nass) regarding their order
C) Because consensus rejected such ranking
D) Because the caliphs were already ranked

Key Takeaway
The six Companions are Talhah, Zubayr, Abdurrahman, Sa'd, Sa'id, and Abu Ubaydah, and their relative superiority is not specified in revelation, so we do not affirm any hierarchy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the Companions, do you find yourself trying to rank them based on your own calculations? How does this lesson's emphasis on relying only on revelation challenge that impulse? Will you commit to leaving their ranks to Allah alone?
?
Are you content with not knowing who among these six is 'better'? Or does that unsettle you? This lesson teaches that silence is the proper stance. Is there an area of your faith where you have assumed a ranking or preference without evidence from revelation? What would it mean for you to surrender that assumption today?
?
If you were among the six Companions, would you be satisfied knowing that your superiority over others is not known? Or would you feel the need to prove yourself? How does this translate to your own desire for recognition in religious matters?

The Ten Promised Paradise: Names and Rank
Enumerates the ten promised Paradise and states the rule of no revealed superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the ten promised Paradise are and why we do not assert superiority among them without a revealed text.

Core Idea
The ten are known by name, but their relative rank is not specified by revelation.

The commentator begins by explaining the terms in the verse that refers to the ten promised Paradise. He then lists the four previous caliphs and the six remaining companions, emphasizing that no text establishes superiority among them. This lesson establishes the basic list and the principle of refraining from ranking without divine warrant.

Guided Path
1
Read the lexical explanations
Notice how the commentator defines each word in the verse (يليهِمُ, قوم, كِرام, بَرَرَهُ). This shows the precision of classical commentary.
2
Identify the ten names
The commentator names the four shaykhs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali) and then the six: Talha, Zubayr, Abd al-Rahman, Sa'd, Sa'id, Abu Ubayda. Commit these names to memory.
3
Understand the principle of no superiority
The commentator states that no text (tawqif) shows superiority among them, so we do not assert it. Reflect on why this is important for avoiding sectarian bias.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَلِيهِمُ قَومٌ كِرامْ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتٌ تَمامُ العَشَرَة قوله : (يَلِيهِمُ بالإشباع ؛ أي: يلي آخرهم وهو عليّ، فالكلام على تقدير مضاف . وقوله : (قوم) أي: رجال . وقوله : (كِرَامٌ) جمع كريم ؛ وهو كريم النفس رفيع النسب. وقوله : (بَرَرَهُ) جمع بَارٌ ؛ وهو المحسن من البر؛ وهو الإحسان. وقوله : ( عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتّ تَمامُ العَشَرة) أي: عددهم سِتَّةٌ تمام العشرة المبشرين بالجنة، فمن جملتهم المشايخ الأربعة السابقون والستة الباقية طلحة بن عبيد الله، والزبير بن العوام، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص وسعيد بن زيد، وأبو عبيدة عامر بن الجراح ولم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض في الأفضلية، فلا نقول به لعدم التوقيف . خالفهم بالزور، وهم خمس فرق كلهم يزعمون أن الأئمة أنبياء محدثون، ورسل الله وحُجَجه على خلقه النبوات وتخصيص هؤلاء العشرة بأنهم مبشرون بالجنة مع أن المبشرين بالجنة أكثر منهم، فإن الحسن والحسين وأمهما فاطمة من المبشرين بالجنة قطعاً؛ لأنَّ هؤلاء العشرة جمعوا في حديث مشهور ففي الترمذي وابن حبان من حديث عبد الرحمن بن عوف عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «أبو بكر في الجنة ، وعمرُ في الجنة، وعثمان في الجنة، وعلي في الجنة، وطلحة في الجنة والزبير في الجنة، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف في الجنة، وسعد بن أبي وقاص في الجنة، وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح في الجنة، وسعيد بن زيد في الجنة
They are followed by a noble people, righteous ones, their number is six, completing the ten. His saying: (يَلِيهِمُ with full vowel) i.e., follows their last, which is 'Ali, so the speech involves an omitted genitive. His saying: (قوم) i.e., men. His saying: (كِرَامٌ) plural of كريم, and he is of noble soul and high lineage. His saying: (بَرَرَهُ) plural of بار, and he is the doer of good from البر (righteousness); it is beneficence. His saying: (عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتّ تَمامُ العَشَرة) i.e., their number is six, completing the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise. Among them are the four previous shaykhs and the remaining six: Talha ibn 'Ubayd Allah, al-Zubayr ibn al-'Awwam, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu 'Ubayda 'Amir ibn al-Jarrah. And no text has come regarding the superiority of some of them over others, so we do not assert it due to lack of a revealed text. Those who oppose them with falsehood are five sects, all of them claiming that the imams are prophets, inspired ones, messengers of God, and His proofs upon His creation. Prophethoods: and the specification of these ten that they are given glad tidings of Paradise, while those given glad tidings of Paradise are more than them, for al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and their mother Fatima are certainly among those given glad tidings of Paradise; because these ten are gathered in a famous hadith: in al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Hibban from the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf from the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'Abu Bakr is in Paradise, 'Umar is in Paradise, 'Uthman is in Paradise, 'Ali is in Paradise, Talha is in Paradise, al-Zubayr is in Paradise, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf is in Paradise, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas is in Paradise, Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah is in Paradise, and Sa'id ibn Zayd is in Paradise.'
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse about the ten promised Paradise. The commentator first explains the terms of the verse: 'they are followed by' refers to the subsequent group after the first four caliphs, i.e., the six who complete the ten. Each term ('قوم', 'كِرام', 'بَرَرَهُ') is defined lexically. Then he lists the ten: the four previous shaykhs (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali) and the six: Talha, al-Zubayr, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu 'Ubayda. He notes that there is no textual evidence for superiority among them, so we do not assert it. Then he mentions five sects that oppose these ten with falsehood, claiming that the imams are prophets, inspired, messengers, and proofs of God. These sects exaggerate the status of the imams. The commentator then clarifies that although others like al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and Fatima are also promised Paradise, these ten are specifically collected in a famous hadith, which he quotes from the Prophet through 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf. The hadith names each of the ten individually, confirming that they are in Paradise.

Key Terms
العشرة المبشرون بالجنة
المشايخ الأربعة
الستة الباقية
التوقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the ten promised Paradise are ranked in order of excellence like the caliphate chronology.
Correction: The commentator explicitly says no text (tawqif) shows superiority, so we do not rank them.

Checkpoint
How many of the ten promised Paradise are explicitly named in this passage?
A) 4
B) 6
C) 10
D) 7

Key Takeaway
The ten promised Paradise are specifically named, but their relative ranks are not specified by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the ranks of these ten companions are not specified by revelation. When you think about them, do you find your heart making subtle judgments about who is better? How can you train yourself to stop such thoughts and leave their status to Allah?
?
The passage teaches you not to assert what has not been revealed. Think of a situation in your life where you have formed an opinion about someone's spiritual rank without clear evidence. How would applying this principle of 'tawqif' change your attitude?
?
You now know the names of the ten promised Paradise. Do you love them all equally as the companions of the Prophet? If not, what is holding you back from giving them the same love that the Prophet's own words guarantee? Examine your heart sincerely.

Refutation of Sects Exalting Imams as Prophets
Refutes sects claiming imams are prophets and explains why the ten are specified in hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the false claim of five sects that imams are prophets and why the ten promised Paradise are especially distinguished in a hadith.

Core Idea
No imam is a prophet; the ten sahaba are uniquely promised Paradise in a sound hadith.

After listing the ten, the commentator confronts sects who exaggerate the status of imams to the level of prophethood. He refutes them by identifying their claim as false and then clarifies that the ten promised Paradise are specifically mentioned in a hadith, even though others like Hasan, Husayn, and Fatima are also promised Paradise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sects' false claim
The commentator says five sects claim that imams are prophets, inspired ones, and proofs of God. This is an exaggeration (ghuluw).
2
Understand the refutation
The commentator does not debate their arguments but labels their claim as falsehood (zur). This shows that such beliefs are categorically rejected.
3
Appreciate the specification of the ten
Even though others are promised Paradise, these ten are collectively mentioned in a famous hadith. This shows their special status without denying Paradise to others.
The hadith is from Tirmidhi and Ibn Hibban.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يَلِيهِمُ قَومٌ كِرامْ بَرَرَهُ عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتٌ تَمامُ العَشَرَة قوله : (يَلِيهِمُ بالإشباع ؛ أي: يلي آخرهم وهو عليّ، فالكلام على تقدير مضاف . وقوله : (قوم) أي: رجال . وقوله : (كِرَامٌ) جمع كريم ؛ وهو كريم النفس رفيع النسب. وقوله : (بَرَرَهُ) جمع بَارٌ ؛ وهو المحسن من البر؛ وهو الإحسان. وقوله : ( عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتّ تَمامُ العَشَرة) أي: عددهم سِتَّةٌ تمام العشرة المبشرين بالجنة، فمن جملتهم المشايخ الأربعة السابقون والستة الباقية طلحة بن عبيد الله، والزبير بن العوام، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف، وسعد بن أبي وقاص وسعيد بن زيد، وأبو عبيدة عامر بن الجراح ولم يرد نص بتفاوت بعضهم على بعض في الأفضلية، فلا نقول به لعدم التوقيف . خالفهم بالزور، وهم خمس فرق كلهم يزعمون أن الأئمة أنبياء محدثون، ورسل الله وحُجَجه على خلقه النبوات وتخصيص هؤلاء العشرة بأنهم مبشرون بالجنة مع أن المبشرين بالجنة أكثر منهم، فإن الحسن والحسين وأمهما فاطمة من المبشرين بالجنة قطعاً؛ لأنَّ هؤلاء العشرة جمعوا في حديث مشهور ففي الترمذي وابن حبان من حديث عبد الرحمن بن عوف عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «أبو بكر في الجنة ، وعمرُ في الجنة، وعثمان في الجنة، وعلي في الجنة، وطلحة في الجنة والزبير في الجنة، وعبد الرحمن بن عوف في الجنة، وسعد بن أبي وقاص في الجنة، وأبو عبيدة بن الجراح في الجنة، وسعيد بن زيد في الجنة
They are followed by a noble people, righteous ones, their number is six, completing the ten. His saying: (يَلِيهِمُ with full vowel) i.e., follows their last, which is 'Ali, so the speech involves an omitted genitive. His saying: (قوم) i.e., men. His saying: (كِرَامٌ) plural of كريم, and he is of noble soul and high lineage. His saying: (بَرَرَهُ) plural of بار, and he is the doer of good from البر (righteousness); it is beneficence. His saying: (عِدَّتُهُمْ سِتّ تَمامُ العَشَرة) i.e., their number is six, completing the ten who were given glad tidings of Paradise. Among them are the four previous shaykhs and the remaining six: Talha ibn 'Ubayd Allah, al-Zubayr ibn al-'Awwam, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu 'Ubayda 'Amir ibn al-Jarrah. And no text has come regarding the superiority of some of them over others, so we do not assert it due to lack of a revealed text. Those who oppose them with falsehood are five sects, all of them claiming that the imams are prophets, inspired ones, messengers of God, and His proofs upon His creation. Prophethoods: and the specification of these ten that they are given glad tidings of Paradise, while those given glad tidings of Paradise are more than them, for al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and their mother Fatima are certainly among those given glad tidings of Paradise; because these ten are gathered in a famous hadith: in al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Hibban from the hadith of 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf from the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'Abu Bakr is in Paradise, 'Umar is in Paradise, 'Uthman is in Paradise, 'Ali is in Paradise, Talha is in Paradise, al-Zubayr is in Paradise, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf is in Paradise, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas is in Paradise, Abu 'Ubayda ibn al-Jarrah is in Paradise, and Sa'id ibn Zayd is in Paradise.'

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse about the ten promised Paradise. The commentator first explains the terms of the verse: 'they are followed by' refers to the subsequent group after the first four caliphs, i.e., the six who complete the ten. Each term ('قوم', 'كِرام', 'بَرَرَهُ') is defined lexically. Then he lists the ten: the four previous shaykhs (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali) and the six: Talha, al-Zubayr, 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Awf, Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, Sa'id ibn Zayd, and Abu 'Ubayda. The Rank and Status of Badr Participants
Commentary on the rank and etymology of Badr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the elevated rank of the people of Badr and that martyrs and survivors among them share the same status.

Core Idea
All participants of Badr share an equal rank, whether they were martyred or not.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the high status of the Companions who fought at Badr. He explains that their rank follows immediately after the six who were promised Paradise during their lifetime. Importantly, he emphasizes that there is no distinction in rank between those who were martyred at Badr and those who survived, correcting a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rank
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the mention of the 'six from the ten' and the list of martyrs.
2
Identify the key points
Look for the statement that there is no difference between martyrs and survivors at Badr, and note the numbers of martyrs from Muhajirun and Ansar.
3
Consider the etymology of Badr
The commentator gives three possible origins for the name 'Badr'. Consider how geographical details are included in such commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَهْلُ بَدْرٍ العَظِيمِ الشَّانِ فَأَهْلُ احُدُ فَبَبْعَةُ الرِّضْوانِ قوله : (فَأَهْلُ بَدْرٍ) بتحريك التنوين للوزن؛ أي: أهل غزوة بدر؛ ففي الكلام تقدير مضاف، فرتبتهم تلي رتبة الستة من العشرة، ولا فرق بين من استشهد فيها، وهم أربعة عشر رجلاً : ستة : ستة من المهاجرين وثمانية من الأنصار وبين من لم يُستشهد فيها . و (بَدْرُ) اسمٌ للوادي أو لبئرٍ فيه بناها رجل في الجاهلية، يقال له: بدر، وفي «السيرة الشامية» : بدر قرية مشهورة على نحو أربع مراحل من المدينة. وكان أهل غزوة بدر ثلاث مئة وسبعة عشر رجلاً، وفي رواية: وثلاثة عشر رجلاً»، ويُؤيد هذه الرواية أنه هو أمر بعدهم فأخبر بأنهم ثلاث مئة وثلاثة عشر ففرح بذلك، وقال : عِدَّة أصحاب طالوت (۲) ، وكان معهم فرسان فقط، إحداهما للمقداد بن الأسود، والثاني للزبير بن العوام، وفي عبارة بعضهم ثلاثة أفراس، وكان أيضاً سبعون بعيراً، وكان المشركون ألفاً ومعهم منة فرس وسبع مئة بعير، وسبق المشركون إلى ماء بدر، فأحرزوه، ولم يصل إليه المسلمون، فعَطشوا وأصبح غالبهم جنباً ، فوسوس الشيطان لبعضهم، وقال تزعمون أنكم على الحق، وفيكم نبي الله ، وأنكم أولياء الله وقد غلبكم المشركون على الماء، وأنتم عطاش، وتصلون محدثين معهم
And the people of Badr of great standing, and the people of Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan. His saying: (فَأَهْلُ بَدْرٍ) with the tanwin moved for the meter; i.e., the people of the Battle of Badr; so in the speech there is an omitted genitive. Their rank follows the rank of the six from the ten, and there is no difference between those who were martyred therein, who are fourteen men: six from the emigrants and eight from the helpers, and between those who were not martyred therein. And (بَدْرُ) is the name of the valley or of a well in it built by a man in pre-Islamic times, called Badr, and in al-Sira al-Shamiyya: Badr is a famous village about four stages from Medina. And the people of the Battle of Badr were three hundred and seventeen men, and in a narration: three hundred and thirteen men. And this narration is supported by the fact that he counted them and informed that they were three hundred and thirteen, so he rejoiced at that, and said: the number of the companions of Talut. And there were only two horsemen with them, one belonging to al-Miqdad ibn al-Aswad and the second to al-Zubayr ibn al-'Awwam, and according to the expression of some, three horses. And there were also seventy camels. And the polytheists were a thousand, with them a hundred horses and seven hundred camels. And the polytheists preceded [the Muslims] to the water of Badr and seized it, and the Muslims did not reach it. So they became thirsty and most of them became junub (in a state of ritual impurity). So Satan whispered to some of them, saying: 'You claim that you are upon the truth, and among you is the Prophet of Allah, and that you are the friends of Allah, yet the polytheists have overcome you at the water, and you are thirsty, and you pray while in a state of impurity with them?'
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the verse mentioning the people of Badr, the people of Uhud, and the Pledge of Ridwan. The commentator explains that 'the people of Badr' refers to those who participated in the Battle of Badr, and their rank is immediately after that of the six from the ten promised Paradise. He notes that there is no distinction among them based on whether they were martyred (14 martyrs: 6 Muhajirun and 8 Ansar) or survived. He then provides a brief etymology of 'Badr': either a valley or a well built by a man named Badr, or a village four stages from Medina. The commentator then gives details about the number of Muslims: 317 men in one narration, and 313 in another, with the latter supported by the Prophet's statement comparing them to the companions of Talut. He mentions that the Muslims had only two horsemen (Miqdad and Zubayr) and seventy camels, while the polytheists numbered a thousand with a hundred horses and seven hundred camels. The polytheists seized the water of Badr, leaving the Muslims thirsty and many in a state of ritual impurity. Satan then whispered to some, questioning how they could claim to be upon the truth with the Prophet among them yet be so disadvantaged.

Key Terms
أهل بدر
المهاجرين
الأنصار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Martyrs of Badr have a higher rank than those who survived.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states there is no difference between them.

Checkpoint
How many martyrs from the Muhajirun (emigrants) were at Badr?
A) Six
B) Eight
C) Fourteen
D) Three hundred seventeen

Key Takeaway
The people of Badr, whether martyred or surviving, hold the second highest rank among the Companions, immediately after the ten promised Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were present at Badr and survived, would you be content with the same rank as a martyr? Why or why not? Examine your heart's attachment to worldly recognition.
?
The lesson says no difference between martyr and survivor. Does your heart truly accept that? Or do you secretly think that a person who dies for the faith is more beloved to Allah? This teaching demands that you surrender your desire for a higher status than others. Can you do that?
?
Badr is named after a valley or a well, not after the moon. What does that teach you about the way Allah elevates the lowly? Is there an area in your life where you seek prestige from something grandiose, when true honor comes from humble service? What step will you take this week to serve invisibly, without seeking recognition?

Details of the Battle of Badr: Numbers, Animals, and the Water Incident
Details of the numbers, animals, and the water incident at Badr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will learn the precise numbers of Muslims and polytheists at Badr, their animals, and the trial of thirst that led to Satan's whisper.

Core Idea
The small number of Muslims at Badr demonstrated divine support against overwhelming odds.

This passage provides historical details about the Battle of Badr: the number of Muslim participants, the few horsemen and camels they had, versus the larger polytheist army. It also describes how the polytheists seized the water, causing thirst and ritual impurity among the Muslims, leading to Satan's whisper questioning their status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on numbers and animals
Read the Arabic text carefully, paying attention to the numbers of Muslims and polytheists, and their respective animals.
2
Note the two narrations

3
Reflect on the water incident
Consider how the polytheists' seizure of water led to thirst and ritual impurity, and how Satan used this to cast doubt.

The Pledge of Ridwan: Loyalty to the Prophet
Narrates the events of the Pledge of Ridwan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circumstances of the Pledge of Ridwan and the companions' unwavering loyalty to the Prophet.

Core Idea
The Pledge of Ridwan showed the companions' readiness to sacrifice everything for the Prophet.

The Treaty of Hudaybiyyah began with a crisis: the Prophet was prevented from entering Makkah and a false rumor of Uthman's death spread. In response, the Prophet called his companions to a pledge of allegiance under a tree, which became known as the Pledge of Ridwan. This lesson narrates the events leading up to that pledge and highlights the companions' devotion.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative of the crisis
Read how the Prophet intended to perform Umrah, was blocked by Quraysh, and sent Uthman as an envoy. Notice the rumor of Uthman's death.
This rumor sparked the pledge.
2
Understand the pledge itself
The Prophet called the companions to pledge allegiance not to flee, and they did so, even placing his left hand for Uthman.
This shows the unity and trust.
3
Note the exception of al-Jadd ibn Qays
Only one man hid from the pledge; he was a hypocrite but later repented. This highlights the sincerity of the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البيت الحرام والاعتمار به ولم يكن معهم سلاح إلا السيوف، فنزلوا بأقصى الحديبية محل معروف فصدهم المشركون عن دخول مكة، فأرسل إليهم عثمان بكتاب لأشراف قريش يعلمهم أنه إنما قدم معتمراً لا مقاتلاً، فقالوا: لا يدخل مكة هذا العام، فشاع أنهم قتلوا عثمان أشاع ذلك إبليس ورفع صوته به، فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام عند ذلك: لا نبرح حتى نناجزهم الحرب، ودعا الناس عند الشجرة للبيعة على الموت، أو على ألا يفروا، بل يصبرون على الحرب، فبايعوه على ذلك، ووضع شماله في يمينه وقال هذه عن يد عثمان أي على تقدير حياته، أو نظراً للحقيقة، ولم يتخلف عنها إلا الجد بن قيس ـ بفتح الجيم ـ اختبأ تحت بطن ناقته، وكان منافقاً، ويقال: إنه تاب وحسن إسلامه، ثم تبينت حياة عثمان. فصالحهم النبي على شروط وهي: أن يوضع الحرب بينه وبينهم عشر سنين، وأن يؤمن بعضهم بعضاً، وأن يرجع في هذا العام ويأتي للعمرة في العام القابل، وأن من جاء ممن تبعه لا يردوه، ومن جاء من قريش مؤمناً يرده، وكره المسلمون ذلك، وقالوا: يا رسول الله؛ إنا نرد ولا يردون، قال: نعم، من ذهب إليهم فأبعده الله، ومن جاء منهم فسيجعل الله له مخرجاً، حتى أسلم أبو جندل وجماعة وانحازوا بجبل يقطعون الطريق على قريش، فأرسلوا له بإسقاط الشروط، وأن يأخذهم عنده. وقد كتب علي: هذا ما صالح عليه محمد رسول الله، فقالوا: لو سلمنا أنك رسول الله ما خاصمناك، فأبى علي أن يمحوها، فقال: أرنيها، فمحاها، وقال: اكتب لهم كما قالوا: محمد بن عبد الله، فإني رسول الله وابن عبد الله، وتحللوا بالحلق والذبح ورجعوا المدينة.
The Sacred House and performing Umrah with it, and they had no weapons except swords. They camped at the far end of Hudaybiyyah, a known place, and the polytheists prevented them from entering Makkah. So he sent Uthman to them with a letter to the chiefs of Quraysh informing them that he had come as a pilgrim, not a fighter. They said: 'He shall not enter Makkah this year.' Then it spread that they had killed Uthman; Iblis spread that and raised his voice with it. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) said at that: 'We will not leave until we engage them in battle.' He called the people at the tree to pledge allegiance to death, or not to flee, but to be patient in war. They pledged allegiance to him on that. He placed his left hand on his right and said: 'This is on behalf of Uthman's hand,' meaning assuming his life, or considering the reality. No one abstained from it except al-Jadd ibn Qays (with fatha on the jim), who hid under the belly of his camel; he was a hypocrite, and it is said he repented and his Islam became good. Then Uthman's life became clear. So the Prophet made peace with them on conditions: that war be suspended between him and them for ten years, that they grant security to each other, that he return this year and come for Umrah the following year, that whoever came to him of those who followed him would not be returned, and whoever came to him of Quraysh as a believer would be returned. The Muslims disliked that and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, we return but they do not return.' He said: 'Yes, whoever goes to them, Allah will remove him; and whoever comes from them, Allah will give him a way out.' Until Abu Jandal and a group embraced Islam and gathered on a mountain, cutting the road against Quraysh. They sent to him to nullify the conditions and that he take them with him. Ali wrote: 'This is what Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah, has agreed upon.' They said: 'If we acknowledged that you are the Messenger of Allah, we would not have disputed with you.' Ali refused to erase it. He said: 'Show it to me.' He erased it and said: 'Write for them as they said: Muhammad ibn Abdullah, for I am the Messenger of Allah and the son of Abdullah.' They then became halal by shaving and sacrificing, and returned to Madinah.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative from the biography of the Prophet, specifically the events of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah. It begins with the Prophet's intention to perform Umrah with his companions, unarmed except for swords. The Quraysh block them, so the Prophet sends Uthman as an envoy. The rumor of Uthman's death leads the Prophet to call for the Pledge of Ridwan (bay'at al-ridwan) under a tree, where the companions pledge to fight to the death. The narrative then mentions that only al-Jadd ibn Qays, a hypocrite, hid from the pledge, but later repented. The treaty conditions are then listed: a ten-year truce, mutual security, postponement of Umrah to the following year, and the controversial clause that Quraysh fugitives who come to the Muslims must be returned, while Muslim fugitives to Quraysh are not returned. The Muslims initially disliked this clause, but the Prophet assured them that Allah would provide a way out. Later, Abu Jandal and others became Muslim and caused trouble, leading Quraysh to request cancellation of that clause. The narrative also highlights the incident where Ali wrote 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' in the treaty document, and the Quraysh objected; the Prophet agreed to write 'Muhammad ibn Abdullah' instead, demonstrating his humility and wisdom. The passage concludes with the Muslims completing the rites of Umrah (shaving and sacrificing) and returning to Madinah.

Key Terms
بيعة الرضوان
الحديبية
الجد بن قيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the pledge was only about fighting and that the companions were bloodthirsty.
Correction: The pledge was a defensive response to the perceived killing of the envoy, emphasizing the importance of protecting the mission of Islam.

Checkpoint
What event triggered the Pledge of Ridwan?
A) The Prophet's dream to perform Umrah
B) The news that Quraysh had killed Uthman
C) The arrival of Abu Jandal
D) The treaty negotiations

Key Takeaway
The Pledge of Ridwan was a powerful demonstration of the companions' loyalty, willingness to sacrifice, and trust in the Prophet's leadership.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The companions were willing to pledge their lives at a moment's notice. If the Prophet were among us today, would you be ready to drop everything and follow him without hesitation, or would you find excuses like al-Jadd?
?
When you hear a rumor about a fellow believer's failure, do you immediately assume the worst, or do you seek the truth as the companions should have done regarding Uthman? Where is your trust in Allah's plan?
?
Al-Jadd ibn Qays hid in shame but later repented. Is there a hidden weakness in your faith that you are avoiding? What specific step will you take today to turn from hiding to wholehearted commitment?

The Treaty of Hudaybiyyah: Conditions and Divine Wisdom
Lists the conditions of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah and the Muslim reaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific conditions of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah and the divine wisdom behind the clause that seemed unfair to the Muslims.

Core Idea
The treaty's conditions, though seemingly one-sided, contained divine wisdom and future victories.

After the Pledge of Ridwan, the Prophet (peace be upon him) entered into negotiations with Quraysh, resulting in a treaty. This lesson outlines the conditions of that treaty, particularly the clause requiring the return of Quraysh refugees, which initially dismayed the Muslims. The Prophet's reassurance reveals the hidden wisdom of Allah's plan.

Guided Path
1
Read the treaty conditions
The passage lists several conditions: a ten-year truce, mutual security, deferment of Umrah, and the controversial return clause. Notice how the Muslims reacted.
2
Understand the Muslim objection
The Muslims objected to the clause that required them to return any Quraysh who came to them, while Quraysh did not have to return Muslim refugees. This seemed unfair.
3
Reflect on the Prophet's response
The Prophet assured them that Allah would provide a way out for those who came, indicating that the treaty was part of a larger divine plan.
This shows the Prophet's trust in Allah.

?

The Treaty Document: Humility and Leadership
Narrates the incident of writing the treaty document and the completion of Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the Prophet's humility in erasing 'Rasul Allah' from the treaty document and the overall wisdom in his leadership.

Core Idea
The Prophet's willingness to compromise on wording showed his priority of peace over pride.

When writing the treaty document, a dispute arose over the Prophet's title. The Quraysh refused to accept 'Muhammad Rasul Allah', and the Prophet agreed to write 'Muhammad ibn Abdullah' instead. This incident, along with the subsequent handling of Abu Jandal's case, demonstrates the Prophet's strategic humility and far-sighted leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the document incident
Ali wrote 'Muhammad Rasul Allah', but Quraysh objected. The Prophet, showing humility, agreed to erase it and write 'Muhammad ibn Abdullah'. Notice how Ali initially refused.
2
Understand the wisdom behind the action
The Prophet's concession was not weakness but a strategic move to secure the treaty and avoid conflict. He affirmed his status privately.
3
Reflect on the outcome
The treaty led to the eventual conquest of Makkah. The Prophet's wisdom in handling the document and the refugee clause proved beneficial.

Why did the Prophet agree to erase 'Rasul Allah' from the treaty document?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you held onto a title, honorific, or public acknowledgment to the point that it hindered a greater good? How does the Prophet's erasing of 'Rasul Allah' challenge you to release your need for recognition today? What is one specific situation this week where you can intentionally let go of being 'right' or 'recognized' to preserve harmony in a relationship?
?
The Prophet said, 'I am the Messenger of Allah and the son of Abdullah'—he affirmed his highest identity when no one else would. Are there areas in your life where you are silent about your faith or identity out of social pressure? What would it look like to quietly but firmly state your truth as the Prophet did, without needing others to endorse it?
?
If you had been in Ali's position, would you have refused to erase the title? What does your emotional reaction to this incident reveal about your pride in defending the Prophet's honor versus your trust in his wisdom? How can you cultivate a heart that trusts the Prophet's decisions more than your own sense of zeal?

The Virtue of the Forerunners: Textual Recognition
The author establishes that the forerunners' virtue is known from Quranic text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the merit of the earliest forerunners is established from the Quran itself, and how the author's grammatical analysis reinforces this point.

Core Idea
The Quran itself declares the virtue of the forerunners; no other proof is needed.

This lesson unpacks the opening line of the poem and its commentary. The author affirms that the merit of the forerunners (al-sabiqun) is textually known from the Quran, citing the verse about the foremost from the Muhajirun and Ansar. The grammatical parsing serves to emphasize that this merit is an established fact, not a matter of opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem line and its grammar
Carefully read the Arabic line and its grammatical analysis. Notice how the author breaks down the sentence structure to show that 'their merit' is the subject and 'known' is the predicate.
The phrase 'nasṣan' (textually) is crucial—it means the merit is derived from explicit Quranic text.
2
Identify the Quranic proof
Find the Quranic verse cited: 'And the foremost forerunners from the Muhajirun and the Ansar...' (9:100). Understand that this verse is the basis for knowing the forerunners' virtue.
3
Reflect on the significance of 'textually'
Ask yourself why the author emphasizes that the merit is 'textually known'. This sets the forerunners' virtue as a matter of divine revelation, not human speculation.
This connects to the broader theme of relying on scripture for theological knowledge.

79. And the forerunners – their merit has been recognized textually, and regarding their identification there has been disagreement. His saying: 'And the forerunners – their merit textually known': this is an independent clause, so it does not come with a conjunction. 'And the forerunners' is the first subject, 'their merit' is the second subject, and the clause 'known' is the predicate of the second subject, and it and its predicate are the predicate of the first subject. 'Textually' is in the accusative due to removing a preposition, and in the expression of some it is accusative as a specification. The meaning: the first predecessors, their merit – meaning abundance of reward over others who do not share this attribute – is known from the text of the Quran or from the direction of the Quran text, such as His saying: 'And the foremost forerunners from the Muhajirun and the Ansar' [9:100] – the verse. And his saying: 'this' i.e., understand this, so it is an object of a deleted verb, and other interpretations are valid. And his saying: 'and regarding their identification there has been disagreement' i.e., regarding the identification of the forerunners the scholars have differed. Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and others among the elders who prayed towards both qiblas (i.e., the qibla of Jerusalem and the Ka'ba) said they are those who prayed to both qiblas, and this is the majority opinion and the most correct. Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi and a group said they are the people of Badr. Al-Sha'bi said they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Thus the opinions are three, the most preponderant being the first. And it is understood from the words of the author that preference is sometimes by individuals and sometimes by categories. The first is like preferring Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthman, then 'Ali. The second is like preferring the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten, then the people of Badr, then the people of Uhud, then the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Some among these ranks may enter into some of them and may enter into all. Thus one may be a forerunner, a caliph, a Badri, an Uhudi, a Ridwani, like the four leading figures. However, 'Uthman is a Badri in reward (1) [Reported by Muslim similarly (1783)] not in presence, because he was appointed by the Prophet over his daughter Ruqayya to nurse her (1), and she died during his absence, and the Prophet said: 'For you is the reward of a man and his share' (2). 'Uthman was nicknamed 'Dhul-Nurayn' for marrying two daughters of the Prophet (peace be upon him): Ruqayya and Umm Kulthum. And it is not known that anyone married two daughters of a prophet besides him.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line from the poem about the forerunners (al-sabiqun). It begins by parsing the grammar of the line, clarifying that 'the forerunners' is the subject, 'their merit' is a second subject, and 'known' is the predicate. The phrase 'textually' is explained as being in the accusative either by removing a preposition or as a specification. The meaning intended is that the superior reward of the earliest Muslims is known from the Quran, citing the verse about the foremost forerunners from the Muhajirun and Ansar. The commentator then addresses the scholarly disagreement over who exactly these forerunners are: some say they are those who prayed to both qiblas (the first and second qibla), which is the most widely accepted view; others say they are the participants at Badr; still others say they are those who gave the Pledge of Ridwan. The commentator notes that the author (al-Laqqani) intended that preference can be given in two ways: by individuals (e.g., Abu Bakr then 'Umar then 'Uthman then 'Ali) or by categories (e.g., the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten promised Paradise, then the people of Badr, etc.). Some individuals may belong to multiple categories, such as the four caliphs being Badri and Uhudi and Ridwani except 'Uthman, who was absent from Badr but still got the reward. The passage ends with a remark about 'Uthman's nickname and his unique status of marrying two of the Prophet's daughters.

المهاجرون
الأنصار

Checkpoint
According to the passage, from where is the merit of the forerunners known?
A) From the consensus of scholars
B) From the text of the Quran
C) From the sayings of the Companions
D) From historical reports

Key Takeaway
The virtue of the earliest forerunners is established directly from the Quran, not from human reasoning or consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran's praise of the forerunners in verse 9:100, does it move you to genuinely admire them, or do you feel distant and disconnected from their example? What one change would bring you closer to their spirit?
?
The author insists their virtue is known textually—not from human opinion. In what areas of your faith do you rely more on what people say than on the clear text of the Quran? Will you commit to revisiting one such area today?
?
If the forerunners' merit is already settled by the Quran, why do you sometimes hesitate to acknowledge the excellence of those who came before you? Is there jealousy or pride in your heart that needs to be rooted out?

Life of Imam al-Bayjuri
intermediate
5 min
Who Are the Forerunners? Three Scholarly Opinions
The author presents three scholarly opinions on the identity of the forerunners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main scholarly opinions on the identity of al-sabiqun and which one is considered most preponderant.

Core Idea
Scholars disagree on the identity of the forerunners; the strongest view is that they are those who prayed to both qiblas.

After establishing that the forerunners' merit is textually known, the author turns to the question of who exactly they are. He presents three opinions from notable scholars: Abu Musa al-Ash'ari's view that they are those who prayed to both qiblas (the most correct), Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi's view that they are the people of Badr, and al-Sha'bi's view that they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. This lesson explores these views and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the three opinions carefully
Identify each opinion and its proponent: Abu Musa al-Ash'ari (both qiblas), Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi (people of Badr), al-Sha'bi (people of Ridwan). Note that the first is said to be the most correct.
The phrase 'those who prayed to both qiblas' refers to those who were Muslim before the change of qibla from Jerusalem to the Ka'ba.
2
Understand the reasoning behind the first opinion
The passage does not give detailed rationale, but it says that Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and 'others among the elders who prayed to both qiblas' held this view. This suggests that the forerunners are those who were among the earliest converts and witnessed both qiblas.
3
Reflect on why the author favors the first opinion
The author states that the first opinion is the majority view and 'the most correct.' Reflect on how this decision is based on the strength of the evidence, though the passage does not elaborate further.
This lesson focuses on the disagreement itself; the merits of each opinion can be explored elsewhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The forerunners are defined by extreme devotion—praying to both qiblas, fighting at Badr, pledging under the tree. Which of these three models challenges you most about your own commitment to Allah? What specific act of devotion will you perform today to draw closer to being among the sabiqun?
?
Scholars disagreed on who the forerunners are, but all agree they were exceptionally devoted. Does your daily life reflect that level of prioritization? If not, what is the one attachment holding you back from being a true forerunner in your time?
?

Ranks of Precedence and the Case of Uthman
The author explains two types of ranking and Uthman's special status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways of ranking the early Muslims (by individuals and by categories), and why Uthman is considered a participant in Badr in reward only.

Core Idea
Precedence is ranked both by individual merit and by group categories; Uthman's absence from Badr does not deprive him of the reward.

This lesson examines the second part of the commentary, where the author explains that preference (tafdil) can be considered in two ways: by individuals (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, etc.) and by categories (the four caliphs, the remaining six of the ten, then the people of Badr, etc.). It also addresses a special case: Uthman, though absent from the Battle of Badr, was granted the reward of a participant because he was tending to the Prophet's ill daughter. This illustrates that merit can be bestowed by divine decree even without physical presence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two modes of preference
Read the passage where the author distinguishes between preference by individuals (e.g., Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, etc.) and preference by categories (e.g., the four caliphs, then the remaining six, then Badr, etc.). Notice that these are overlapping classifications.
Some individuals belong to multiple categories, e.g., the four caliphs are also Badri, Uhudi, Ridwani.
2
Examine the case of Uthman
Focus on the explanation that Uthman is a Badri in reward (ajran) not in presence (huduran) because he was sent by the Prophet to care for his daughter Ruqayya who was ill. He missed the battle but was given the reward and share.
This is based on a hadith reported by Muslim.
3
Reflect on the significance of Uthman's nickname
Uthman is called 'Dhul-Nurayn' (possessor of two lights) because he married two daughters of the Prophet, a unique honor. This underscores his high status despite his absence from Badr.
No other prophet had a son-in-law who married two of his daughters.

٧٩ - وَالسَّابِقُونَ فَضْلُهُمْ نَمَّا عُرِف هَذَا وَفِي تَعْبِينِهِمْ قَدِ اخْتُلِفْ قوله : (والسَّابِقُونَ فَضْلُهُمْ نَصَّا عُرِف : هذه جملةٌ مستأنفة، ولهذا لم يأتِ بحرف الترتيب، (والسابقون) مبتدأ أول، و(فضلهم) مبتدأ ثان، وجملة قوله : (عرف) خبر المبتدأ الثاني، وهو وخبره خبر عن المبتدأ الأول ؛ و(نصا) منصوب على نزع الخافض، وفي عبارة بعضهم : منصوبٌ على التمييز والمعنى: والمتقدمون الأولون فضلهم بمعنى كثرة ثوابهم على غيرهم ممن لم يشركهم في هذه الصفة، عُرف من نص القرآن أو من جهة نص القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْمُهَاجِرِينَ وَالْأَنصَارِ [التوبة: ١٠٠] الآية. وقوله : (هَذَا ) أي : افهم هذا ، فهو مفعول لمحذوف، ويصح غير ذلك. وقوله : ( وفِي تَعْيِينِهِمْ قَدِ اخْتُلِف أي: وفي تعيين السابقين قد اختلف العلماء، فقال أبو موسى الأشعري وغيره من الأكابر الذين صلوا إلى القبلتين؛ أي: قبلة بيت المقدس والكعبة، وهذا هو قول الأكثر ، وهو الأصح ، وقال محمد بن كعب القرظي وجماعةٌ هم أهل بدر، وقال الشعبي هم أهل بيعة الرضوان، فالأقوال ثلاثة أرجحها أولها، وقد عُلم من كلام الناظم أن التفضيل تارةً يكون باعتبار الأفراد، وتارة يكون باعتبار الأصناف. فالأول كتفضيل أبي بكر ثم عمر ثم عثمان ثم عليّ، والثاني كتفضيل الخلفاء الأربعة، ثم الستة الباقية من العشرة ، ثم أهل بدر، ثم أهل أحد، ثم أهل بيعة الرضوان، وبعض أهل هذه المراتب ربما دخل في بعضها، وربما دخل في الجميع، فقد يكون سابقاً خليفةٌ بدريًا أُحديًّا رِضوانيًا كالمشايخ الأربعة، لكن عثمان بدري أجراً (1) أخرجه مسلم بنحوه (۱۷۸۳). لا حضوراً ؛ لأنه خَلَّفَهُ على بنته رُقَيَّةَ يُمرِّضُها (١) ، وماتت في غيبته ، وقال : لك أجر رجل وسهمُه : (۲) ، وكان عثمان يُلقب بذي النورين لتزوجه ببنتيه صلى الله عليه وسلم رقية وأم كلثوم، ولم يُعلمُ مَنْ تزوج بنتي نبي غيرُه .
79. And the forerunners – their merit has been recognized textually, and regarding their identification there has been disagreement. His saying: 'And the forerunners – their merit textually known': this is an independent clause, so it does not come with a conjunction. 'And the forerunners' is the first subject, 'their merit' is the second subject, and the clause 'known' is the predicate of the second subject, and it and its predicate are the predicate of the first subject. 'Textually' is in the accusative due to removing a preposition, and in the expression of some it is accusative as a specification. The meaning: the first predecessors, their merit – meaning abundance of reward over others who do not share this attribute – is known from the text of the Quran or from the direction of the Quran text, such as His saying: 'And the foremost forerunners from the Muhajirun and the Ansar' [9:100] – the verse. And his saying: 'this' i.e., understand this, so it is an object of a deleted verb, and other interpretations are valid. And his saying: 'and regarding their identification there has been disagreement' i.e., regarding the identification of the forerunners the scholars have differed. Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and others among the elders who prayed towards both qiblas (i.e., the qibla of Jerusalem and the Ka'ba) said they are those who prayed to both qiblas, and this is the majority opinion and the most correct. Muhammad ibn Ka'b al-Qurazi and a group said they are the people of Badr. Al-Sha'bi said they are the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Thus the opinions are three, the most preponderant being the first. And it is understood from the words of the author that preference is sometimes by individuals and sometimes by categories. The first is like preferring Abu Bakr, then 'Umar, then 'Uthman, then 'Ali. The second is like preferring the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten, then the people of Badr, then the people of Uhud, then the people of the Pledge of Ridwan. Some among these ranks may enter into some of them and may enter into all. Thus one may be a forerunner, a caliph, a Badri, an Uhudi, a Ridwani, like the four leading figures. However, 'Uthman is a Badri in reward (1) [Reported by Muslim similarly (1783)] not in presence, because he was appointed by the Prophet over his daughter Ruqayya to nurse her (1), and she died during his absence, and the Prophet said: 'For you is the reward of a man and his share' (2). 'Uthman was nicknamed 'Dhul-Nurayn' for marrying two daughters of the Prophet (peace be upon him): Ruqayya and Umm Kulthum. And it is not known that anyone married two daughters of a prophet besides him.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line from the poem about the forerunners (al-sabiqun). It begins by parsing the grammar of the line, clarifying that 'the forerunners' is the subject, 'their merit' is a second subject, and 'known' is the predicate. The phrase 'textually' is explained as being in the accusative either by removing a preposition or as a specification. The meaning intended is that the superior reward of the earliest Muslims is known from the Quran, citing the verse about the foremost forerunners from the Muhajirun and Ansar. The commentator then addresses the scholarly disagreement over who exactly these forerunners are: some say they are those who prayed to both qiblas (the first and second qibla), which is the most widely accepted view; others say they are the participants at Badr; still others say they are those who gave the Pledge of Ridwan. The commentator notes that the author (al-Laqqani) intended that preference can be given in two ways: by individuals (e.g., Abu Bakr then 'Umar then 'Uthman then 'Ali) or by categories (e.g., the four caliphs, then the remaining six of the ten promised Paradise, then the people of Badr, etc.). Some individuals may belong to multiple categories, such as the four caliphs being Badri and Uhudi and Ridwani except 'Uthman, who was absent from Badr but still got the reward. The passage ends with a remark about 'Uthman's nickname and his unique status of marrying two of the Prophet's daughters.

Key Terms
الخلفاء الأربعة
العشرة المبشرون
ذو النورين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that those who missed Badr cannot be considered among the forerunners.
Correction: Uthman's case shows that absence due to a valid prophetic mission does not diminish one's reward; he is still counted among the forerunners in merit.

Checkpoint
Why is 'Uthman considered a Badri in reward rather than presence?
A) He was killed before Badr
B) He was tending to the Prophet's daughter Ruqayya
C) He was a child during Badr
D) He was the only one given this status

Key Takeaway
Uthman's unique circumstances illustrate that divine reward can be granted for obedience and sacrifice even when physical presence is lacking.

?

?

The Four Imams and Their Virtues in Hadith
Praises the four imams and mentions the hadiths indicating their virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four imams of fiqh are praised through hadiths that are applied to them but remain speculative in proof.

Core Idea
The hadiths praising the imams are applied, not explicit.

This lesson explores the commentary on a line of poetry that lauds the four imams. The commentator first explains the grammar, then delves into the hadiths traditionally cited to support their virtue. Importantly, he notes that these hadiths are not specific or definitively authentic, teaching us the balance between veneration and scholarly caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once. Then read the commentary to understand the context and analysis.
Pay attention to the grammatical breakdown of the opening line.
2
Identify the three hadiths
Find the three hadiths cited in the passage. Note how each is traditionally applied to a specific imam (Malik, al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa).
The hadiths are referenced by numbers in parentheses in the Arabic.
3
Understand the speculative status
Notice the commentator's statement that each of these hadiths is 'dhanni' (speculative). Consider what this means for the certainty of the evidence.
This is a key point of the passage: the hadiths are not definitively authentic or specific.
4
Reflect on the balance
Think about why it is important to praise the imams while also acknowledging the limitations of the hadith evidence. How does this reflect scholarly integrity?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صلَّى الله على سيِّدنا مُحمَّد
فضل هداة أمة سيدنا محمد
(١) أئمة الفقه الأربعةُ
وَمَالِكٌ وَسَائِرُ الأَئِمَّة كَذَا أَبُو القَاسِمُ هُدَاهُ الأُمَّة

قوله : (ومَالِكُ) مبتدأ . وقوله : (وسَائِرُ الأَئِمَّة) عطف عليه، والخبر قوله : (هداة الأمة). وأما قوله : (كَذَا أَبُو القَاسِمُ جملة معترضة بين المبتدأ والخبر.
واعلم أنه لم يصح في الأئمة الأربعة حديث ،بالخصوص، وإنما ورد «يُوشك أن تُضربَ أكباد الإبل يطلبون العلم فلا يجدون أحداً أعلم من عالم المدينة» (۳)، فحمل على الإمام مالك، فكانوا يزدحمون على بابه لطلب العلم، وقيل: هو كل عالم منها ، وورد «عالم قريش يملأ طباق الأرضِ عِلماً » (٤) ، فحمل على الإمام الشافعي، وقيل: هو ابن عباس وورد «لو كان العلم بالثريا لناله رجالٌ من فارس (٥)»، فحمل على أبي حنيفة وأصحابه وكلٌّ من هذه الأحاديث ظني.
May Allah bless our master Muhammad. The virtue of the guides of the nation of our master Muhammad. (1) The four imams of jurisprudence. And Malik and the rest of the imams, likewise Abu al-Qasim, are the guides of the nation. His statement: (And Malik) is a subject. And His statement: (And the rest of the imams) is coordinated with it, and the predicate is His statement: (guides of the nation). As for His statement: (Likewise Abu al-Qasim) is an interpolated sentence between the subject and predicate. And know that there is no specific authentic hadith regarding the four imams, but there has come: 'Soon the livers of camels will be struck seeking knowledge, and they will not find anyone more knowledgeable than the scholar of Madinah' (3), so it was applied to Imam Malik, and they would crowd at his door for knowledge. It is also said: it refers to any scholar from Madinah. And there came: 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the layers of the earth with knowledge' (4), so it was applied to Imam al-Shafi'i, and it is also said: it is Ibn Abbas. And there came: 'If knowledge were at the Pleiades, men from Persia would attain it' (5), so it was applied to Abu Hanifa and his companions. And each of these hadiths is speculative (dhanni).
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry praising the four imams of fiqh. The commentator begins with a blessing on the Prophet. Then he introduces the heading: 'The virtue of the guides of the nation of our master Muhammad' and then quotes the line: 'And Malik and the rest of the imams, likewise Abu al-Qasim, are the guides of the nation.' He then provides a grammatical analysis: 'Malik' is the subject, 'and the rest of the imams' is a coordinate, and the predicate is 'guides of the nation'. The phrase 'likewise Abu al-Qasim' (i.e., Imam al-Shafi'i, whose kunya is Abu al-Qasim) is an inserted sentence. Next, he makes an important point: there is no single authentic hadith that specifically and exclusively mentions all four imams by name. Rather, there are hadiths that have been interpreted as referring to them. He cites three hadiths: (1) 'Soon the livers of camels will be struck...' which is applied to Imam Malik, though some say it refers to any scholar of Madinah. (2) 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the layers of the earth with knowledge' — applied to al-Shafi'i or Ibn Abbas. (3) 'If knowledge were at the Pleiades...' — applied to Abu Hanifa and his companions. He then states that each of these hadiths is dhanni (speculative, not definitive) in their proof. This shows that the veneration of these imams is based on general indications rather than explicit, decisive texts. The commentator thus balances praise with scholarly caution regarding hadith authenticity.

Key Terms

هداة الأمة
حديث ظني
أبو القاسم
عالم المدينة
عالم قريش
رجال من فارس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: There are explicit, authentic hadiths that specifically name each of the four imams individually.
Correction: The passage clarifies that no such specific hadith exists; the hadiths cited are applied by scholars through interpretation and are not definitive in their proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the hadiths that are used to praise the four imams?
A) They are all authentic (sahih) and specific
B) They are all fabricated (mawdu')
C) Each of them is speculative (dhanni) and not specific to one imam exclusively
D) They are all from the category of mutawatir

Key Takeaway
The veneration of the four imams is supported by hadiths that are speculative in authenticity and open to interpretation, not by explicit texts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the hadiths praising the imams are speculative in their authenticity, does that shake your confidence in their authority? How do you reconcile this with your trust in their scholarship? What does this teach you about the nature of religious knowledge and certainty?
?
You have likely heard the hadith about the scholar of Madinah and assumed it definitely refers to Imam Malik. After learning it can also refer to any scholar of Madinah, how does this challenge your previous understanding? Are you willing to accept ambiguity in matters of virtue?
?
The commentator balances praise with caution. In your daily life, do you ever attribute definitive status to statements about scholars without verifying? What can you do today to approach religious teachings with more scholarly humility?

Hadiths Supporting the Four Imams
Commentator explains that no specific hadith names all four imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the scriptural support for the four imams comes from general hadiths interpreted to refer to each, not from specific hadiths naming them.

Core Idea
The four imams are supported by general hadiths, not specific ones.

This passage clarifies a common misconception: there is no single hadith that mentions all four imams by name. Instead, scholars have applied three well-known hadiths to Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Abu Hanifa respectively. Each hadith is general and its application is based on interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the overall claim that no specific hadith exists.
2
Identify the three hadiths and their imams
Find each hadith in the passage and note which imam it is applied to. The hadiths are: the scholar of Medina (Malik), the scholar of Quraysh (al-Shafi'i), and men from Persia (Abu Hanifa).
The same hadith may have alternative interpretations.
3
Note the commentator's remark on reliability
Observe that all three hadiths are described as speculative (zanni). Consider why this matters for the strength of the evidence.
Speculative hadiths do not give certainty but are still used in scholarly support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لم يصح في الأئمة الأربعة حديث ،بالخصوص، وإنما ورد «يوشك أن تُضربَ أكباد الإبل يطلبون العلم فلا يجدون أحداً أعلم من عالم المدينة» (٣)، فحمل على الإمام مالك، فكانوا يزدحمون على بابه لطلب العلم، وقيل: هو كل عالم منها ، وورد عالم قريش يملأ طباق الأرضِ عِلماً » (٤) ، فحمل على الإمام الشافعي، وقيل: هو ابن عباس وورد لو كان العلم بالثريا لناله رجالٌ من فارس (٥)، فحمل على أبي حنيفة وأصحابه وكلٌّ من هذه الأحاديث ظني.
And know that there is no specific hadith that has been authenticated concerning the four imams specifically. Rather, it has been reported: 'Soon the flanks of camels will be struck seeking knowledge, and they will not find anyone more knowledgeable than the scholar of Medina' (3). This was interpreted as referring to Imam Malik, and they would crowd at his door seeking knowledge. It is also said: he is every scholar from it. And it has been reported: 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the layers of the earth with knowledge' (4). This was interpreted as referring to Imam al-Shafi'i, and it is said: he is Ibn Abbas. And it has been reported: 'If knowledge were at the Pleiades, men from Persia would attain it' (5). This was interpreted as referring to Abu Hanifa and his companions. And all of these hadiths are speculative.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage discusses the scriptural basis for the distinction of the four imams. The commentator states that there is no hadith specifically mentioning all four by name. Instead, three hadiths are used: one about the scholar of Medina (applied to Malik), one about a scholar of Quraysh (applied to al-Shafi'i or Ibn Abbas), and one about men from Persia attaining knowledge (applied to Abu Hanifa and his school). The commentator notes that these hadiths are all speculative (zanni), i.e., not definitively established.

The Inclusion of Other Scholars among the Guided Ones
Commentator explains that 'sā'ir al-a'imma' can include more than the four, listing many notable scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'the rest of the imams' can include many scholars beyond the four famous ones, and you will be familiar with several notable figures mentioned in the commentary.

Core Idea
The 'rest of the imams' includes many scholars beyond the four, showing a broad tradition.

This passage from the commentary clarifies the meaning of 'sā'ir al-a'imma' in the poem. While some restrict it to the four well-known imams, the commentator argues for a broader interpretation that includes many other luminaries. Let's see which scholars are mentioned and why they are included as guided ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the list of names and the commentator's reasoning for including them.
Pay attention to the distinction between the two interpretations presented.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentator first presents a restrictive view (only the four imams) and then advocates for a broader view that includes others.
3
Note the quoted scholars
Identify each scholar mentioned, along with any brief remark or quote that highlights their status.
There are at least seven scholars mentioned beyond the four.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the commentator includes this list: to show the breadth of scholarship that follows the guided path.
This reinforces the poem's emphasis on following the righteous predecessors.

وقوله : (وسَائِرُ الأَئِمَّة ) أي : باقيهم ، فـ (أل) في ((الأئمة) للعهد، والمعهود الأئمة الأربعة فقط، والأولى جعلها للكمال لا بقيد عهد الأربعة فقط، فيَدْخُلُ حينئذ على كل من الاحتمالين الإمام الشافعي أبو عبد الله محمد بن إدريس، والإمام أبو حنيفة النعمان بن ثابت والإمام أحمد بن حنبل، ويدخل على الاحتمال الثاني الليث بن سعد، وداود الظاهري، فإنه كان جبلاً في العلم وما نُقل عن إمام الحرمين من أنه لا يُؤخذ بكلام الظاهرية ولا يُعوّل عليه .. فمحمول على طائفة مخصوصة كابن حزم، ويدخل أيضاً : سفيان الثوري، وكان يُسمّى أمير المؤمنين في الحديث. و إسحاق بن راهويه . و محمد بن جرير الطبري. و سفيان بن عيينة، وكان يقول : (إذا كانت نفس المؤمن محبوسة عن مكانها الجنة بدَيْنِهِ حتى يُقضى فكيف بصاحب الغيبة؟ فإن الدَّيْنَ يُقضى، والغيبة في لا تُقضى) (1) . وعبد الرحمن بن عمر الأوزاعي، وكان يقول : ليس ساعة من ساعات الدنيا إلا وتعرض على العبد يوم القيامة، فالساعة التي لا يذكر الله فيها تتقطع نفسه عليها حسرات، فكيف إذا مرت ساعة مع ساعة ويوم مع يوم؟
And His saying: 'and the rest of the imams' – meaning the remainder of them. So the 'al' in 'al-a'imma' is for the covenant, and the intended meaning is only the four imams. But it is better to make it for perfection without the restriction of only the four. Then, on either possibility, it includes Imam al-Shafi'i Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Idris, and Imam Abu Hanifa al-Nu'man ibn Thabit, and Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal. And on the second possibility, it includes al-Layth ibn Sa'd and Dawud al-Zahiri, for he was a mountain of knowledge. And what has been transmitted from Imam al-Haramayn that the statements of the Zahiris are not to be taken nor relied upon is to be understood as referring to a particular group like Ibn Hazm. It also includes: Sufyan al-Thawri, who was called the Commander of the Faithful in Hadith; and Ishaq ibn Rahawayh; and Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari; and Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, who used to say: 'If the soul of a believer is held back from its place in Paradise because of his debt until it is paid, then what about the one guilty of backbiting? For the debt can be paid, but backbiting cannot be paid.' (1); and 'Abd al-Rahman ibn 'Amr al-Awza'i, who used to say: 'There is no hour of the hours of this world but it is presented to the servant on the Day of Resurrection. As for an hour in which he does not remember Allah, his soul will be cut off in regret over it. So what if an hour passed after an hour and a day after a day?'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage expands on the phrase 'the rest of the imams' from the poem. The commentator discusses whether it refers only to the four well-known imams or to other scholars as well. He prefers the broader interpretation, which includes many other notable scholars. He then lists several examples: al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Dawud al-Zahiri, Sufyan al-Thawri, Ishaq ibn Rahawayh, Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari, Sufyan ibn 'Uyaynah, and 'Abd al-Rahman al-Awza'i. For each, he provides a brief remark or quote demonstrating their status.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'the rest of the imams' only refers to the four well-known imams (Abu Hanifa, Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad).
Correction: The commentator explains that the phrase can be interpreted broadly to include many other scholars, such as al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Dawud al-Zahiri, Sufyan al-Thawri, and others.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'sā'ir al-a'imma' in the poem is interpreted broadly to include many scholars, demonstrating the wide scope of those who follow the guided path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a list of great scholars. How many of their teachings have you actually sought out or implemented in your life? Which one will you make an effort to learn from this week, and what specific step will you take?
?
Al-Awza'i said every hour not spent remembering Allah will bring regret. Think about your last 24 hours: which hours will cause you the most regret? What will you change tomorrow to ensure those hours are filled with remembrance?
?
Ibn 'Uyaynah compared backbiting to an unpaid debt that cannot be settled. Is there someone you have backbitten that you have not yet made amends with? What concrete action will you take today to seek their forgiveness or rectify the harm?
